```
On Jul 29, 1:59 pm, "William M. Klein" <wmkl...@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
wrote:
> Vince,
>   Just to clarify,
…[58 more quoted lines elided]…
> - Show quoted text -

All:

In my migration practice I mostly migrate batch and CICS systems from
zOS and VSE to various Linux variants, although I am looking at taking
on a Windows system, as well.  With one exception the systems have
been procedural COBOL/CICS/DB2 to UniKix/Oracle or MySQL.  For the
past couple of years I have been using a commercial product which DOES
support object COBOL, as well as various RDBMSs.  My current effort is
with such a system that will not use a CICS replacement, but, rather,
will use a servlet container (Glassfish) with the servlet wrapper
written in object COBOL to handle the presentation chores.  This
product has a well-developed IDE which has a very good debugger.  I am
evaluating COBOL-IT and Open COBOL, but it looks like they just are
not yet ready for prime time.

The COBOL source is mostly IBM Enterprise COBOL, with some COBOL-II
and COBOL-D thrown in just to keep things interesting.  My biggest
problems revolve around the JCL facilities like GDGs and IDCAMS.  XML
handling and out-of-language calls are handled by the product, which
compiles to Java.  This last feature allows me to use any Java class
as though it were native, which means that I can also call REXX
scripts (using BSF4REXX).  Naturally, I can use HTML and JSPs (both
static and dynamic), due to the Java compatibility, and there is a
screen-definition facility as well, although I do not use it.

I have used MF since it was CIS-COBOL, and I began to use Acu just
before the purchase, and I find this product to meet my needs better.
This product also is supported far better than the MF complex.  I just
can't understand why it is not named frequently (or at all) on this
newsgroup.  The deficiencies noted in this and other threads just
mystify me;  I know the product to be successful, so why do I not hear
more about it?  It certainly does meet some of the unfilled needs
expressed here.
```

```

Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message
news:h4nh5m05j0@news1.newsguy.com...
> In matters of English usage, one may be a descriptivist or
> prescriptivist, and to varying degrees. But prescriptivism that
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> doubt there are many real cases where it would remove any ambiguity).

"Fuse" is either a verb, or a name for the thing in the box that melts and
interrupts the current when it gets too high.  A "fuze" is what sets off an
artillery shell.

> The same can't be said of "meter" and "metre" in any context I know of.
>

Well, you've already admitted that in other countries the distinction is
retained.  That ought to be a recognizable context.

But let me put a question to you (whether it's "descriptivist or
prescriptivist" I'll leave to you).  If useage is the determinant, how do
you feel about:

wander/wonder
your/you're
are/our

and my personal bugaboo:

uninterested/disinterested

I routinely see these words used interchangeably.  Persons whose spelling
skills are poor mix the fiirst three pairs up based on pronunciation,
although to an Englishman the two are distinct (but the fourth is just lack
of vocabulary).  Is the evolution of the language to be in the direction of
less and less precision, or a lack of knowing what the speaker/writer is
actually trying to say?  I'm not going to say that metre/meter is a crucial
example but I would like to know where you draw the line; also whether you
feel that you're representative of any significant group.

Respectfully,
Peter
```

```
tlmfru wrote:
> Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message
> news:h4nh5m05j0@news1.newsguy.com...
…[50 more quoted lines elided]…
> Peter

It's really quite amazing, but over the last 10 years or so, I have become 
really tolerant of bad spelling and grammar. I no longer feel personally 
affronted by someone else's lack of interest in it or lack of education. 
(There are a huge number of good folks who never had the benefit of a decent 
education, and there are an equally huge number of students today who are 
not getting a decent education in English, either.) I think constant 
exposure just enures us.

All of the above examples would once have evinced a caustic reaction from 
me; now they don't. Even the one I hated most: "You shouldn't of..." (I hate 
this phrase at so many levels I won't even start... but, just in case we 
have some English students who are wondering what is "incorrect" about it, 
it should be:"You shouldn't have..." Please feel free to smack anyone who 
says "you shouldn't of.." (or "you should of...") in the mouth, with my 
compliments... :-))

I think it is because I have been doing a fair amount of editing of writing 
(my own and others) lately, and I have been doing more serious writing and 
reading.

At the end of the day, language is for communication, and, although there is 
pleasure in the sheer elegance of good English, if the message gets through, 
there is little point in being disappointed by the wrapper.

Nevertheless, I shall continue to endeavour not to personally contribute to 
the further decline of  the language of Shakespeare, Byron, Keats, Tennyson, 
Shelley, Edgar Allen Poe, Rudyard Kipling, Damon Runyon, Winston Churchill, 
Arthur Conan Doyle, Walter Scott, Robert Louis Stevenson, Herman Melville, 
Henry Wadsworth Longfellow, to name just a few of my favourites...

(I was a bit shocked to notice there are no women in the above list. I tried 
to think of a female writer whose work I enjoy... Pam Ayres. Sad isn't it... 
? :-))

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: English how he/she is written

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2009-08-03T12:00:08-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<h571pr023ds@news1.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<I8mdm.100471$qx1.13440@newsfe04.iad>`
- **References:** `<db1ab610-f312-4745-8ecd-f1ec5d95ddff@h11g2000yqb.googlegroups.com> <fPOdnd5yPJc46v3XnZ2dnUVZ_radnZ2d@earthlink.com> <h3pur0$ehn$2@news.eternal-september.org> <6048m.17995$Qg6.4992@newsfe14.iad> <h3r1u6$i9j$2@reader1.panix.com> <VNr8m.23946$Qg6.268@newsfe14.iad> <h3u3cm$rja$2@news.eternal-september.org> <iYS8m.15553$O23.2788@newsfe11.iad> <h43cqj01p49@news3.newsguy.com> <Jwn9m.23072$O23.9857@newsfe11.iad> <h4nh5m05j0@news1.newsguy.com> <I8mdm.100471$qx1.13440@newsfe04.iad>`

```
tlmfru wrote:
> Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message
> news:h4nh5m05j0@news1.newsguy.com...
>> In matters of English usage, one may be a descriptivist or
>> prescriptivist, and to varying degrees.

>> The same can't be said of "meter" and "metre" in any context I know of.
> 
> Well, you've already admitted that in other countries the distinction is
> retained.  That ought to be a recognizable context.

I don't think I have admitted that, actually; but it's moot, because
that's not a context where "meter" and "metre" are terms of art.

In US English, a distinction is typically made between "blue" (used
for the color, among other things) and "bleu" (used to describe a
variety of cheese). But "blue cheese" is also frequently used,
synonymously with "bleu cheese", and even in contexts where "bleu
cheese" might be a term of art (eg cheesemaking) the distinction
doesn't appear to be meaningful.

As far as I know, the same applies to "meter" and "metre": there are
no English-language contexts where the two terms have different
technical meanings, and substituting one for the other makes any
semantic difference.

> But let me put a question to you (whether it's "descriptivist or
> prescriptivist" I'll leave to you).  If useage is the determinant, how do
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> are/our

For all these, and other homonymic substitutions, I prefer the
preferred or traditional form (what a prescriptivist would call the
"correct" form). This is a different case from variant spellings (such
as "meter" / "metre", "color" / "colour", etc); here two words with
different, well-established meanings and spellings (since English
spelling was regularized in the Early Modern era) are clearly being
confused.

Descriptivists and prescriptivists of various stripes can all agree on
what's preferred in cases like this. The difference is that the
prescriptivist describes it in terms of correctness - positing rules
for English usage - while the descriptivist does so in terms of
preference.

> and my personal bugaboo:
> 
> uninterested/disinterested

This is a third case - confusion of two similar words with
traditionally different meanings.

It's possible to have a descriptive view of English and take a
prescriptive stance: you can say that English is defined by, and
evolves through, usage, but go on to promulgate a set of rules for
using it. Those rules might appeal to etymology or regularity or
various other principles, or they might simply be stated as your
personal preferences. (The Augustans thought making English more like
Latin was a good principle, for example, which is where we got the
absurd dictum against the "split infinitive", still upheld by many as
holy writ.)

Those prescriptive rules might be taken up by others as
recommendations; in some cases, they can even be enforced to some
extent, for example by editors.

I personally recognize that the descriptive position on the nature of
English is correct, but that doesn't prevent me from trying to
maintain a fairly rigorous set of rules for my own spoken and written
usage. I don't regularly critique others' usage, because there's
little reason to do so; but when I taught college composition, for
example, I did my best to explain my preferences and the reasons
behind them, and to indicate when I thought it likely that other
readers would share them.
```

```
Doug Miller wrote:
> In article <h571pr023ds@news1.newsguy.com>, Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote:
> 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> force it into a Latin grammar model is absurd.

Agreed, though the Norman influence from Middle English on is so
strong that it might be more accurate to call English "a language with
a Germanic base" or something like that.

But the Augustans didn't think English should be more like Latin
because it was a Romance language; they thought it should be more like
Latin because Latin was inherently superior. They might as well have
picked Greek or Hindi or Japanese. (Think how much trouble we'd be
saved if they'd reduced English to only two tenses, like Japanese. All
those irregular past-tense forms gone...)

Of course my point really was that a lot of the usage rules which
hapless schoolchildren were forced to memorize, such as the
prohibition on split infinitives, are supported by only the flimsiest
of arguments.

That's literally the case for one of my favorites - the rule (still
widely observed by editors in the US, less so elsewhere) that periods
(full stops) and commas be moved inside adjacent closing quotation
marks, even when they're not logically part of the quoted text.
Apparently that arose as a way to protect those small pieces of lead
type, back when it was still set by hand. Flimsy indeed.
```

```
On Sun, 2 Aug 2009 14:50:14 -0500, "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote:

>"Fuse" is either a verb, or a name for the thing in the box that melts and
>interrupts the current when it gets too high.  A "fuze" is what sets off an
>artillery shell.

Is that a British thing?   I'm not near my OED, but on-line
dictionaries seem to use "Fuse" as both.    Ahh, but not the on-line
OED.

http://www.onelook.com/?loc=pub&w=fuse

http://education.yahoo.com/reference/dictionary/entry/fuse has an
interesting distinction:

 NOUN:

   1. A cord of readily combustible material that is lighted at one
end to carry a flame along its length to detonate an explosive at the
other end.
   2. often fuze A mechanical or electrical mechanism used to detonate
an explosive charge or device such as a bomb or grenade: "A mechanical
. . . switch is used to initiate the fuzes" (International Defense
Review).


(Interesting, my American spell checker for this newsreader doesn't
recognize "fuze")
```

```
"I was browsing through an old copy of my alumni magazine, and I'm old

Donald Lewis Tees, B.Sc. '75.  On October 7, 2008 in New Hamburg,
Ontario, aged 61.

this
is our own Don Tees, who used to have a snooker table in his loft and

in
heaven, Don.  Smoke 'em if you got 'em.

Dean "

RIP Donald.

PatH
```

```
On Jul 29, 6:11 am, "William M. Klein" <wmkl...@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
wrote:
> How are the 49th and 50th characters defined in your record layout?  Is it a
>   USAGE COMP
…[247 more quoted lines elided]…
> read more »

Hi..

I figured out a round about solution for this..

well.. the code is not exactly for work.. it was more for analysis
part ..

Using a unix script, i changed the characters 49-50 from CRLF to ~~.
And, Since, there is not CR LF anymore at 49-50th bytes, the code for
line sequential file now reads past 50th character.

The code was only for analysis and therefore, i didnt have a need to
take care of the efficiency part of the pgm.

Thank you all!!
```

## Thread: AIX - MVS — 4 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### AIX - MVS

- **From:** gaya3 <gayathri.kalyanasundaram@wipro.com>
- **Date:** 2009-08-04T20:54:37-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<f862c3d8-b66d-4174-9cd4-89f7e74e1652@r36g2000vbn.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hi all,

There are two ways to view a dataset in MVS.
The normal mode where we see just the data and
the HEX mode wherein we can see what the data is and also its HEX
equivalent.

Whereas in AIX,
The vi editor seems to have a provision only to view the data in
normal mode.
IS there a way to view the data in AIX in HEX mode as in MVS?
Is there any other way to view the HEX equivalent in AIX.

Thanks to all!
Gaya3
```

```
For example the Midnight Commander can do it.

http://www.perzl.org/aix/index.php?n=Main.HomePage




gaya3 wrote:

> Hi all,
> 
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> Gaya3
```

#### ↳ Re: AIX - MVS

- **From:** Fred Mobach <fred@mobach.nl>
- **Date:** 2009-08-05T12:35:01+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<h5bn8n$isi$1@news.dmz.bit.nl>`
- **References:** `<f862c3d8-b66d-4174-9cd4-89f7e74e1652@r36g2000vbn.googlegroups.com>`

```
gaya3 wrote:

> The vi editor seems to have a provision only to view the data in
> normal mode.

That's not correct, the next command shows the data in hexadecimal and
character mode :
%!xxd
Example :

0000000: 5468 6520 7669 2065 6469 746f 7220 7365  The vi editor se
0000010: 656d 7320 746f 2068 6176 6520 6120 7072  ems to have a pr
0000020: 6f76 6973 696f 6e20 6f6e 6c79 2074 6f20  ovision only to
0000030: 7669 6577 2074 6865 2064 6174 6120 696e  view the data in
0000040: 0a6e 6f72 6d61 6c20 6d6f 6465 2e0a 4953  .normal mode..IS
0000050: 2074 6865 7265 2061 2077 6179 2074 6f20   there a way to
0000060: 7669 6577 2074 6865 2064 6174 6120 696e  view the data in
0000070: 2041 4958 2069 6e20 4845 5820 6d6f 6465   AIX in HEX mode
0000080: 2061 7320 696e 204d 5653 3f0a 4973 2074   as in MVS?.Is t
0000090: 6865 7265 2061 6e79 206f 7468 6572 2077  here any other w
00000a0: 6179 2074 6f20 7669 6577 2074 6865 2048  ay to view the H
00000b0: 4558 2065 7175 6976 616c 656e 7420 696e  EX equivalent in
00000c0: 2041 4958 2e0a 0a                         AIX...


To revert to character mode only :
%!xxd -r

> IS there a way to view the data in AIX in HEX mode as in MVS?

Don't know about MVS.

> Is there any other way to view the HEX equivalent in AIX.

Yes, that's what the od command can do for you. You might read the
manpage or the PDF file at
http://publib.boulder.ibm.com/infocenter/pseries/v5r3/topic/com.ibm.aix.cmds/doc/aixcmds4/aixcmds4.pdf
```

```
Fred Mobach wrote:

…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> %!xxd

That requires the xxd executable, which is not supplied with AIX. It's
normally installed as part of the vim distribution; vim is a fine
enhanced vi, but it isn't standard on AIX.

Standard vi does not include a feature for viewing data in hexadecimal.

>> Is there any other way to view the HEX equivalent in AIX.
> 
> Yes, that's what the od command can do for you.

And that *is* part of AIX. It can even be used as a filter in standard
vi, though it's irreversible unless you undo the change without making
any intervening changes.
```

---

```
I was video chatting to isCOBOL yesterday. The product is impressive and I 
think we'll be hearing more from them.

You are right, Dan. This is a very good product and it integrates with the 
Java environment very well.

Questions about one language being "better" than another are becoming less 
and less relevant.

All that matters is functionality. If it is encapsulated as an object, then 
the language the object is written in is neither here nor there.

The days when Source code was King and had to be lovingly protected and 
worhipped in the inner sanctum, are gone for anyone using Objects.

Pete..
```

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> "Mattc" <mpcsoft6@gmail.com> wrote in message
> news:2ee72528-cf4e-42d6-b972-172fffb7a920@32g2000yqj.googlegroups.com...
…[51 more quoted lines elided]…
> two compare in functionality?

I had not heard of it either until I received an email suggesting video 
conference. But I suspect that is going to change. isCOBOL have written 
their own COBOL compiler and it supports OO. I understand it generates Java 
which then runs in the JVM, so the platform is not too important. We spoke 
about the possibility of generating other targets like CLR for .NET and I 
don't think it would be too hard for them to do this. isCOBOL is a COBOL 
development environment where you can develop COBOL objects as well as 
standard COBOL.

As I have been saying for some time now, the future is in Objects and 
Layers. I believe they are a dynamic company, eager to make their presence 
felt. I feel sure we'll be hearing more from them.

PerCOBOL, on the other hand, is a means of converting COBOL code to Java 
with an intention of migrating away from COBOL. I don't think it is the same 
thing at all, but I haven't used either of these so I don't claim expertise 
in them.

At least, the above is my understanding. (I would welcome correction if 
needed :-))

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: Micro Focus product questions

- **From:** Rene_Surop <infodynamics_ph@yahoo.com>

```
This is definitely annoying 'most' MF customers. Anyway, I will try to
at least answer what 'I knew' about their policy.

>
> 1. What does MF charge for MF Net Express 4.0 COBOL ide?
>    Where can I get it for less?
>    MUST apps developed with this product ALWAYS be run on MF run-time?
You can ask this to ONLY Microfocus product reseller, NOT from
Microfocus office. And the price may vary. See, this is what I do not
like about buying NetExpress. Reseller will be the one to give you the
price (and support if any). Concerning deployment; yes, you will
always need MF run-time for your apps.

>
> 2. What does MF charge for MF 4.0 Application Server (run time)
> license?
This is what you're going to spend big! Depends on how many users and
how big is your application server. MF 'only' price application
servers (I think) because run-time client apps can be copied anytime
and with the right configurations.

>
>    Where can I get it for less?  Short-term need only.
>
No such thing as 'less' in MF though it is worth it. And to tell you
honestly, their IDE is really powerful.

>
> 3. Is Microfocus EVER responsive?
One thing that they're somewhat lacking. They have lots of FAT clients
to attend to that is why, and their Consultants are scarce.

So basically, the first step is to look for Microfocus Product
Reseller and talk to them about your requirements so that they will be
able to give you the 'right' tool/product you need.
```

```
  * * * *

The IDE is NOT required for development, but it certainly is useful.  To the 
best of my knowledge, they have never (and don't) sell it separately, if that is 
what you are asking about?  (They now have both a VisualStudio plug-in and a 
non-VS IDE.  This, of course, assumes you are developing on Windows.  There are
```

```
This sounds like what we used to call 'parallel testing'.  In my 
experience Doing It Right involves a lot of work, time, money and people; 
doing it... Less-Than-Right requires less up front but more later on, when 
folks find out that the semi-annual processes are going kerflooie or the 
year-end close-outs are out of balance.

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Micro Focus product questions

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2009-08-07T11:11:16-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gtno75d4hio3l1jmdoa0jlp3qcel8r44m0@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<badbee65-608b-4bc7-b805-4ee05eee3bf0@26g2000yqk.googlegroups.com> <U1Bem.419137$2p1.397194@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com> <ec94f247-11f4-4e74-b216-7866a77c2324@p15g2000vbl.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Fri, 7 Aug 2009 07:01:52 -0700 (PDT), DocDay
<Richard.Day@eTelic.com> wrote:

>We're working with a client who has a legacy COBOL application running
>on Microfocus 4.0 Application Server. They develop using Microfocus
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>purposes. We're looking to accomplish that at a minimum of expense.

The main difficulty with such parallel testing is that the data are
often different.    It is expensive, but very necessary to have users
spend a lot of time comparing data from the old way with data from the
new way.    Programmers can only tell if the programs work as designed
- not whether the design works with 20-20 hindsight.
```

```
contractually signed, temporary license, and at a reduced price, would 
be dependent upon Micro Focus.

Whether the current system uses COBOL files or a DBMS and depending upon 
what you are proposing to use, the best you can do is write a series of 
programs comparing files/tables record by record, (or row by row), 
between the old and new applications. And even that approach can be 
fraught with danger, subject to conditions which may arise in your test 
data.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Micro Focus product questions

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2009-08-08T06:01:06-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<TVcfm.480356$Xo1.51731@en-nntp-07.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<badbee65-608b-4bc7-b805-4ee05eee3bf0@26g2000yqk.googlegroups.com> <U1Bem.419137$2p1.397194@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com> <ec94f247-11f4-4e74-b216-7866a77c2324@p15g2000vbl.googlegroups.com>`

```
As Jimmy indicated, I am not a Micro Focus employee (and haven't been for over a 
decade).  I do have "contacts" still at MF and I am glad that this lead to some 
contact.

Now, for what I *think* you are asking for, it sounds like you ONLY want to run 
an "existing" application on your machine.  If that is the case, then the 
"owner" (assuming they are current on support payments) of the existing 
application should be able to purchase a "run-time license" to port the existing 
application to your system.  It doesn't sound as if you need to actually do ANY 
"development" on your system.

It would seem to me that the company whose application you are "migrating" 
should be able to bare most (if not all) of the cost of such a run-time license. 
(However, this may depend on your contract with them.)

As others have pointed out, it is unlikely that MF would offer you a "deal" on a 
system to migrate AWAY one of their existing customers from their product.  On 
the other hand, a purchase of a "run-time license" for copying an existing MF 
application to a new machine should be a REASONABLY priced option.

  * * * * *

It might be of interest to find out if the existing MF customer only runs the 
existing application on one machine.  If not, they SHOULD have multiple run-time 
licenses already and SHOULD know how to "port" it to another (similarly O/S and 
environment) machine.

Obviously (???) if you are actually providing a "migration" to another 
environment (Operating system, file-system, whatever), then you will need to 
"rebuild" the existing MF application in order to do a "true" parallel test. 
This will certainly be more expensive (for you and/or your customer)  and may 
require expertise AND software that you don't currently have.
```

<a id="t-ae36c5671e"></a>
## Thread: SEARCH table without INDEXED BY phrase in IBMEnterprise COBOL? — 2 msgs

### SEARCH table without INDEXED BY phrase in IBMEnterprise COBOL?

- **From:** mmehlich <michael@mehlich.com>
- **Date:** 2009-08-06T16:43:10-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a3572810-d0ed-4fca-8d32-59d53f52a14f@p15g2000vbl.googlegroups.com>`

```
I found the following phrasing in the IBM Enterprise COBOL language
reference:

The data description entry for identifier-1 should contain an OCCURS
clause with the INDEXED BY phrase, but a table can be searched using
an index defined for an appropriately described different table.

I couldn't find any example describing how this is supposed to work?
Does anyone know what to write in order to search a table without
INDEXED BY phrase? How does the compiler determine which index to use?

Thanks,
  Michael
```

#### ↳ Re: SEARCH table without INDEXED BY phrase in IBMEnterprise COBOL?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-08-07T21:21:05+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7e2a02F2e0897U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<a3572810-d0ed-4fca-8d32-59d53f52a14f@p15g2000vbl.googlegroups.com>`

```
mmehlich wrote:
> I found the following phrasing in the IBM Enterprise COBOL language
> reference:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>  Michael

Sure, you can have any number of tables of the same "shape", and only ONE of 
them needs to be indexed. (I would strongly discourage you from doing this, 
it isn't recommended practice, and just because you CAN do something does 
not mean that you SHOULD... :-))

01 Table1.
      12 tab1Entries occurs 30
           indexed by t1-x1.
           15 t1-part1   pic x(10).
           15 t1-part2    pic x(20).

01 Table2.
      12 tab2Entries occurs 30.
           15 t2-part1   pic x(10).
           15 t2-part2    pic x(20).


01 Table3.
      12 tab3Entries occurs 30.
           15 t3-part1   pic x(10).
           15 t3-part2    pic x(20).


Then you can say...

      TAB1-search.
              set t1-x1 to 1
              search tab1entries
                   AT END
                          some action...
                   WHEN
                          some action...
              end-search

Note the search makes no reference to the index...

Now search Table2 (which has no index defined for it...)

      TAB2-search.
              set t1-x1 to 1
              search tab2entries
                         varying t1-x1
                   AT END
                          some action...
                   WHEN
                           t2-part1 (t1-x1) = ws-search-key
                                move t2-part2 (t1-x1) to t3-part2 (t1-x1)
              end-search

Note the same Table1 index can reference Table2 AND Table3, but, in my 
opinion it is foolish to do it.

While this is legal, it is not pretty and it very quickly becomes confusing. 
For the sake of an "indexed by" clause, make sure that all your tables have 
their own indexes... it really is much easier.

Pete.
```

```
> a test with the OS/VS COBOL compiler. (I don't care if you use the original 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

Bill,

Up until about 3 years ago I would have been able to help you with this, 
but my shop permanently removed the OS/VS COBOL compiler.  The story I 
heard was that it continued to be included with the basic OS/390 and 
z/OS install "tapes" (even though support was dropped in 1994), because 
we were still licensed to use it.  I used to be able to run OS/VS batch 
COBOL compiles as long as I steplib'ed or joblib'ed to a special library 
where that compiler lived.  Not any more.

We're still finding the occasional OS/VS COBOL load module, but without 
any evidence that they are still running.
```

## Thread: Reading JCL as data — 1 this month / 6 total

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

_Thread started [2009-07](2009-07.md#t-ff100c604c)._

#### ↳ Re: Reading JCL as data

- **From:** monkie <nospam@nospam.com>
- **Date:** 2009-08-16T14:16:28-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<EPGdnaNtap5q0RXXnZ2dnUVZ_oidnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<7c9vmaF26sg3rU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<7c9vmaF26sg3rU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> In Oldene Tymes when prehistoric IBM mainframes stalked the corridors of 
> Corporate, and spinning platters were starting to provide random access to 
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

Comment...I did that migrating batch cobol (and JCL) from legacy to 
Windows/NT

(OS)RUN batchfile (a .bat file, equivalent to a JCL file because both 
are 'native' command files)

(batchfile) SET MYPARM=%0.bat

(batchfile) RUN cobolprogram

(cobol) ACCEPT batchfilename FROM MYPARM (environment label/name)

then OPEN and READ it LINE SEQUENTIAL

Another method is

(batchfile) RUN cobolprogram %0

which passes the name of the executing, native batch (JCL) file name to 
the cobol program as a parameter, rather than fetching it from an 
environment variable.  With either method, the physical name of the 
jcl/batch file can change without maintenance to the code.  If you run 
cobol from a modern script language instead (not a native OS batch 
command language) then the script will have to provide equivalent 
capability as the native OS, as provided in command.com on Windows/NT. 
I don't know unix, but I assume it's various 'native' batch facilities 
can do it.
```

<a id="t-552876ebed"></a>
## Thread: specifying z/OS load libraries — 29 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### specifying z/OS load libraries

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2009-08-18T12:40:20-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4A8AA134.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
Dear Fellow Cobol Programmers,

This is not Cobol specific, but I think that there are many more z/OS
programmers in here than on the ibm-main list...

I'm going to stir the pot again, as I seem to often do.  What can I say;
it's important to me.

One of my big nitpicks with z/OS versus VSE is in VSE we have, for 30 years,
specified "system level" definitions for what libraries are to be searched
(in a particular LPAR/VM guest image) to locate both application executables
and system executables.  Here is an example of such a system level
definition:

LIBDEF PHASE,SEARCH=(THIRD.TCPIP,PRD1.BASE,PRD2.SCEEBASE,PRD2.DB2750,  X
               PRD2.DLI1A0,THIRD.SORT,PRD2.PSF,PRD2.PROD,PRD2.AFP,     X
               USER.PROD),PERM  

This is executed at IPL, specifying the "permanent search chain" for all
jobs that don't otherwise somehow override it.

Things of specific interest are PRD2.DB2750 which is DB2/VSE V9.5,
PRD2.DLI1A0 which is DL/I VSE V1.10, and USER.PROD which contains all
"application load modules".

This makes a simple production JCL look something like this:

// JOB MYJOB
// EXEC PGM=MYPGM
/&

MYPGM is an executable in USER.PROD.  It may call SUBR1 and SUBR2, which are
both executables in USER.PROD.  Perhaps it also uses DB2.  It gets those
modules from PRD2.DB2750.  All is good.

Let's say I want to change MYPGM and I'd like to test it (always a good
idea).  I compile/link it in to USER.FJS.  Now here is my "test" JCL:

// JOB MYJOB
// LIBDEF PHASE,SEARCH=(USER.FJS)
// EXEC PGM=MYPGM
/&

The libraries in the LIBDEF specified in the job are searched first, and if
a module is not found there the operating system will then search through
the "PERM" libraries.  So in this case the version of MYPGM in USER.FJS is
executed.  SUBR1 and SUBR2 are in USER.PROD and not in USER.FJS so the prod
version are executed.  Again, all is good!

So let's go to z/OS.  Let's say we are like (apparently) most z/OS shops
that don't have application loadlibs in LNKLST.  So here is production JCL:

//MYJOB    JOB
//JOBLIB   DISP=SHR,DSN=PROD.APPL.LOAD
//STEP01   PGM=MYPGM
//

Hmm, that's OK.  Don't love it, but it's OK.  Now we'd like to test changes
as above.  So now we have:
//MYJOB    JOB
//JOBLIB   DISP=SHR,DSN=FJS.APPL.LOAD
//         DISP=SHR,DSN=PROD.APPL.LOAD
//STEP01   PGM=MYPGM
//

Well now I'm getting irritated.  I not only had to add "//JOBLIB  
DISP=SHR,DSN=FJS.APPL.LOAD", which I have no problem with, but I also had to
keep the existing JOBLIB of PROD.APPL.LOAD *except* I had to remove the
JOBLIB name from it so that it's concatenated to the end of the new JOBLIB. 
Yes, yes, petty, but it just shouldn't be necessary!

On the other hand, if PROD.APPL.LOAD is in the LNKLST all is good:

Production JCL:
//MYJOB    JOB
//STEP01   PGM=MYPGM
//

Test JCL:
//MYJOB    JOB
//JOBLIB   DISP=SHR,DSN=FJS.APPL.LOAD
//STEP01   PGM=MYPGM
//

Much nicer!  When I want to execute a "test" module I add a JOBLIB.  When I
don't I have nothing (no JOBLIB or STEPLIB).

In our new z/OS LPAR where we are testing our migration we currently do
have, after a bit of prodding of systems, our application loadlibs in
LNKLST.  There were two additional considerations we had to deal with.  We
had to change our IEASYSxx system parms module from having LNKAUTH=LNKLST to
instead have LNKAUTH=APFTAB.  This is because if you have the former than
anything in the LNKLST because by default an "authorized library" when
accessed via the LNKLST concatenation.  With APFTAB this does not occur.

The other thing we did is we added an LLA REMOVE command to CSVLLAxx so that
our application libraries are *not* in the LLA.  Specifically (member
CSVLLAAP):
REMOVE(EMER.APPL.LOAD,PROD.APPL.LOAD)
We could have chosen to leave them in the LLA, but that would mean doing a
refresh of the LLA each time we make a change to this libraries, and that
didn't seem to have a good cost/benefit return.

Do any of your z/OS shops out there utilize LNKLST for applications
libraries?  I realize many of you are consultants and really have no say how
a particular shop is set up.  Then again, you also probably have experience
with many different ways.  What do you prefer?  What heartaches do certain
methods cause you?

Don't bother telling me it's a petty concern.  I've been told that enough
times already.  :-)

Anyway, I was pretty happy with going down this path.  I then asked a
question on the IMS listserv and someone responded that not only should we
not have application libraries in LNKLST, but we shouldn't even have IMS
system libraries in it!  So now production JCL might look like this:

//MYJOB    JOB
//JOBLIB   DISP=SHR,DSN=PROD.APPL.LOAD
//         DISP=SHR,DSN=IMS.V10.SDFSRESL
//STEP01   PGM=MYPGM
//

His point is that we might have a case where we'd, in one LPAR, want to have
some applications to point to one version of IMS and others to point to
another.  Knowing my shop as well as I do (13+ years in applications
development) I don't think we will ever want to support two version of IMS
(or DB2) in the same LPAR.  That being said I am curious from an
applications point of view what kind of JCL is used to support this
possibility.

In the end, assuming I give up my LNKLST dream, I am thinking I would have
something like this:

//MYJOB    JOB
//         INCLUDE APPL1
//         INCLUDE APPLIBS
//         INCLUDE SYSLIBS
//STEP01   PGM=MYPGM
//

And another job might be a member of "application 2" and look like this:
//APP2JOB  JOB
//         INCLUDE APPL2
//         INCLUDE APPLIBS
//         INCLUDE SYSLIBS
//STEP01   PGM=APP2JOB
//

Here are the "include" members

APPL1 member
//         SET DFSVER=V10
//         SET DSNVER=V910

APPL2 member
//         SET DFSVER=V10
//         SET DSNVER=V910

JOBLIBS member
//JOBLIB   DD DISP=SHR,DSN=PROD.APPL.LOAD

SYSLIBS member
//         DD DISP=SHR,DSN=PROD.APPL.DFSRESL
//         DD DISP=SHR,DSN=IMS.&DFSVER..SDFSRESL
//         DD DISP=SHR,DSN=DSN.&DSNVER..SDSNEXIT
//         DD DISP=SHR,DSN=DSN.&DSNVER..SDSNLINK
//         DD DISP=SHR,DSN=DSN.&DSNVER..SDSNLOD2
//         DD DISP=SHR,DSN=DSN.&DSNVER..SDSNLOAD

For a test job I could then have
//MYJOB    JOB
//         JCLLIB ORDER=(FJS.APPL.PROC)
//         INCLUDE APPL1
//         INCLUDE APPLIBS
//         INCLUDE SYSLIBS
//STEP01   PGM=MYPGM
//

And in FJS.APPL.PROC I would then have a JOBLIBS member:
//JOBLIB   DD DISP=SHR,DSN=FJS.APPL.LOAD
//         DD DISP=SHR,DSN=PROD.APPL.LOAD

Does all of this seem reasonable?  As much as I've argued for use of LNKLST,
this might be something I can live with.  But I'd love to see examples of
how other shops deal with this.

Thanks!
Frank
```

#### ↳ Re: specifying z/OS load libraries

```
Frank,
  I wonder what your PROCs look like?  In most MVS-ish shops that I worked on 
PROCs were set up with symbolic to handle "commonly" modified STEPLIBs (more 
often than JOBLIBS).

On the other hand, "testing" JCL was like COBOL programs; programmers simply 
"copied and changed" existing JCL to do what they want.

I certainly do agree that placing "application" load libraries in LNKLIST is 
"unusual" at best (and probably NOT a good idea).  Doesn't that require them to 
be APF authorized?  (It has been so long, I can't remember if this is true or 
not).
```

- **References:** `<4A8AA134.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <4MCim.553508$6p1.299000@en-nntp-02.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
>>> On 8/18/2009 at 12:52 PM, in message
<4MCim.553508$6p1.299000@en-nntp-02.dc1.easynews.com>, William M.
Klein<wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com> wrote:
> Frank,
>   I wonder what your PROCs look like?  In most MVS-ish shops that I 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> "copied and changed" existing JCL to do what they want.

Can you give me an example of what this might look like?

As for the test JCL, the "copy and change" method is what we use now on VSE.
 We were hoping (and perhaps we'll be disappointed) that we could use PROCs
with symbolics to eliminate most of this.  So rather than the simple example
I gave originally we might have production JCL that looks like this:

//MYJOB   JOB
//JOBLIB  ...whatever goes here
//PROC01  EXEC PROC=MYPROC
//

Then for a test job we'd leave MYPROC untouched, and simply change MYJOB,
perhaps like this:
//MYJOB   JOB
//JOBLIB  ...whatever goes here
//PROC01  EXEC PROC=MYPROC,ENV1=DEVA,ENV2=DEVB,ENV3=DEVC
//

It may end up being hopeless, so that we end up having to make too many
changes in test jobs for this to work, but I think it's a worthy goal.  The
closer that production JCL is to the JCL used to test it the less likely
things are to get screwed up.
 
Unrelated, but I am also curious as to why one would prefer a STEPLIB to a
JOBLIB, especially when all of the steps in the job load from the same
library.  Seems to me that STEPLIB is just a lot of extra typing (or cut and
paste at the very least).

> I certainly do agree that placing "application" load libraries in 
> LNKLIST is 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> not).

With regard to APF auth, as I mentioned in the original message, systems can
specify LNKAUTH=APFTAB (instead of LNKAUTH=LNKLST).  If this is done then
only those specifically selected with an APF ADD statement become APF
authorized libraries.  All others do not, even when accessed via the LNKLST
concatenation.  (I have tested this out.  It is true.)

Thanks!
Frank
```

- **From:** Arnold Trembley <arnold.trembley@att.net>

- **References:** `<4A8AA134.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <4MCim.553508$6p1.299000@en-nntp-02.dc1.easynews.com> <4A8AF66F.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
Frank Swarbrick wrote:
>(snip)
> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> I gave originally we might have production JCL that looks like this:

Frank, I'm not sure if anything I say will be any help, because I am 
somewhat clueless about LNKLST and other things that our sysprogs manage 
for us.

For a number of reasons, my shop is encouraging the use of symbolics in 
JCL, and I dislike them.  It does make it easier to test production JCL 
in our test environments, but it also makes production JCL harder to 
read when trying to figure out why a batch job went down at 3 AM.

In my shop, the jobs look something like this:

//MYJOB   JOB (whatever goes in the jobcard)
//SYSAFF  CPU=(whatever)
//JOBLIB   DD DSN=HLQ.PROD.PGMLIB,DISP=SHR
//PROC01  EXEC PROC01,P1=HLQ

A typical batch job where I work only has one library in the JOBLIB DD. 
  But I have seen as many as five DSN's under the JOBLIB DD, for DB2 or 
certain vendor products or extremely specialized applications.

We have three test environments before PROD (don't ask why), and they 
are called DTF (Development Test Facility), ITF (Integrated Test 
Facility), and CERT.  So if I need to build JCL for the DTF, the JOBLIB 
DD looks like this:

//JOBLIB   DD DSN=DTF.PROD.PGMLIB,DISP=SHR
//         DD DSN=ITF.PROD.PGMLIB,DISP=SHR
//         DD DSN=CERT.PROD.PGMLIB,DISP=SHR
//         DD DSN=HLQ.PROD.PGMLIB,DISP=SHR
//PROC01  EXEC PROC01,P1=HLQ

If the names seem funny, that's because they aren't exactly the real 
names, and believe it or not, in each test environment we have TEST and 
PROD libraries for that environment.  So there is both a DTF.TEST.PGMLIB 
and a DTF.PROD.PGMLIB.  By convention, we use PGMLIB as the final node 
of our application load library names instead of LOAD or LOADLIB.  That 
was probably an arbitrary choice made nearly 30 years ago.

I remember during the runup to Y2K, I fixed and tested a date routine 
that was a dynamically called assembler subprogram.  Then I tried to get 
it installed into production.  To my surprise I found it did not live in 
HLQ.PROD.PGMLIB, but in a LNKLST library owned by the sysprogs, named 
something like SYS1.LINKLIB3, so my install required an LLA refresh.  I 
hardly knew what an LLA refresh was at the time, because Application 
programmers shouldn't have to think about it.  So installing this one 
little subprogram became almost as complicated as scheduling a POR and 
IPL.  I think they had to stop all production batch processing to do the 
LLA refresh, and that was quite a scheduling problem.

If it had been in the HLQ.PROD.PGMLIB with all the other applications 
programs, it would have been a snap.

> (snip)
>  
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> paste at the very least).

I don't mind the typing, and there are a number of advantages to using 
STEPLIB.  For example, you want to test a new version of a dynamic 
subprogram in one step of a job but not all the steps.  I would add a 
STEPLIB DD to that step only.  Basically, you use STEPLIB if you want a 
different library search order for a single step in a multi-step job.

We typically do not use STEPLIB in production jobs, because it's rarely 
needed.  But I don't think we have any standards to discourage us from 
using STEPLIB in production jobs.

>  (written by William Klein)
>> I certainly do agree that placing "application" load libraries in 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>> not).

The standard where I work is to keep application load libraries out of 
LNKLST.  That decision was made decades ago and I was not consulted. 
Since this is the only MVS shop I have worked in, I can't even be sure 
how common that is in other MVS or z/OS shops.

With kindest regards,
```

```
//AJOB382P JOB (AB1000,4025,OTHRSTUF),TEST,MSGCLASS=R,
```

```
>> When running PROD then yes, all the load-modules should come from the 
>> same 
>> library/libraries; when testing a STEPLIB can be rather helpful.

>> //STEPNAME.STEPLIB DD DSN=HINODE.TEST.LOAD,DISP=SHR

…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>//         DD DSN=HINODE.PROD.LOAD,DISP=SHR

Now we are descending into the Realm of the Systems Jockey and I have to 
say 'I'm not really sure'.  I vaguely recall being taught, away back when, 
that depending on how the system is gen'd there is a default set of 
libraries that are searched for the existence of a load module; this is 
the reason that one should not have to code a JOBLIB or STEPLIB for stuff 
like an IEFBR14.

This 'default' list should be what you see after issuing a TSO LISTALC 
command from the ISPF command line (or simply LISTALC from the TSO READY 
prompt... but I would say System Jockies worth their salary will not allow 
anyone besides themselves to get that close to the bare metal).

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: specifying z/OS load libraries

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2009-08-20T18:33:50-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4A8D970E.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`
- **References:** `<4A8AA134.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <4A8AF66F.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <h6h44n$kug$1@reader1.panix.com> <4A8C451D.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <h6jh6a$vm$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
>>> On 8/20/2009 at 6:56 AM, in message <h6jh6a$vm$1@reader1.panix.com>,
<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote:
> In article <4A8C451D.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>,
> Frank Swarbrick <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote:
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
> like an IEFBR14.

There is.  It's called the LNKLST.  It has mostly "systemish stuff" in it
(like whatever library IEFBR14 is in), but generally (from what I've heard)
does not have application libraries in it.
 
> This 'default' list should be what you see after issuing a TSO LISTALC 
> command from the ISPF command line (or simply LISTALC from the TSO READY 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> anyone besides themselves to get that close to the bare metal).

Those are the libraries allocated to your TSO session, but not necessarily
the libraries that are allocated to a batch job (and in fact as far as I can
tell the LNKLST libraries don't appear to be returned by LISTALC at all). 
If you want to see what libraries you can execute jobs from without the need
for JOBLIB or STEPLIB, go to SDSF and type:
/D PROG,LNKLST

Frank
```

```
All I gotta do, eh?  There's a career for you in Management, Mr 
Swarbrick... after a bit of diddling about I discovered that my system is 
gen'd so that if one wishes to EXECute anything not defined in the System 
Standard libraries then the stuff-to-be-executed (CLIST, REXX, etc) must 
be in a dataset named in a certain manner.

For example: TSO EXEC LUIGI gets me DATA SET USERID.LUIGI.CLIST NOT IN 
CATALOG OR CATALOG CAN NOT BE ACCESSED.

When I allocate USERID.LIB.CLIST and create member IPLINFO (as downloaded 
from the link) everything works just fine... oh, did I mention that on 
this particular system you cannot define a VSAM dataset unless the second 
node is VSAM?  USERID.KSDS.MSTRFIL will not be permitted, it has to be 
USERID.VSAM.MSTRFIL.

The moral of this exegesis is: there are exegeses without morals.

DD
```

```
>DD

As Doc has aptly pointed out, not all sites trust their people to run
their own CLIST or REXX statement.  I've been in sites that you only
needed to allocate a PDS following their programmer naming protocal
with the word CLIST as the 3rd node.  Then the next time you logged
in, it would be added to the REXX/CLIST search string as the first
location.  You could then put all the CLISTS and/or REXX members you
wanted in there and be able to run them.

I've been in sites that on the other extreme not wont add a private
CLIST/REXX PDS to your search string, they disable the TSO xxxx where
xxxx is a REXX member in your own PDS.

So, all you gotta do is.........

Regards,
```

- **References:** `<4A8AA134.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <4A8F8E02.2020102@att.net> <Pine.NEB.4.64.0908241553390.8589@panix2.panix.com> <4A92E2F9.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <h70rnt$8ic$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> (snip) 
> All I gotta do, eh?  There's a career for you in Management, Mr 
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

So, what happened when you ran IPLINFO?  Were you able to get the 
LNKLST?  Did you find out interesting things about your mainframe 
hardware and Operating System?  Did it give you the MSU rating for your CEC?

I would post the results from my shop, but it's considered company 
confidential, and I do not want to tempt fate.

Hmmm... I've never defined a VSAM file here named USERID.NODE2.NODE3, 
but I doubt there is any restriction on the second node.  Generally my 
VSAM files have names like TEST1.XX.TREMBLEY.WHATEVER, where XX is a 
required corporate application ID (think of AP for Accounts Payable, for 
example, although we don't have that one).  Only the first two nodes of 
the Dataset Name have required values for RACF/ACF2 security.

The really curious thing is that all VSAM files here have to begin with 
  TEST1, TEST2, PROD1, or PROD2 because ages ago there used to be two 
separate VSAM catalogs.  We haven't used that type of VSAM catalog since 
the early 1990's but the naming convention lives on.  Non-VSAM files (or 
PDS's) would simply have TEST or PROD for the first node of the dataset 
name, without a 1 or a 2.
```

```
><9b2m85935hqs6jo6g0g77dderf3b2mfi1e@4ax.com>, Howard
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>Frank

I started out as a VSE/VM applications and system programmer but have
been on MVS for about 11 or 12 years now solely as an application
programmer.  I work at multiple MVS shops in a years time but one
thing they all had in common was their library structure.  

System programs were kept in their own library; 3rd party utilities in
their own and all applications were kept in one library, normally
called PROD.MAIN or some derivative of that.   All of these libraries
are defined at IPL time in a permanent JOBLIB and do not need to be
repeated for production.

For testing, because all of my clients use multi-level testing
environments, you must override the PROD.MAIN application library
level by using a temp JOBLIB that lists your test library first
followed by PROD.MAIN.  This really isn't much different than  the
LIBDEF SEARCH= statement you listed for VSE.

So I guess I'm wondering what exactly the issue is?

Regards,
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: specifying z/OS load libraries

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2009-08-19T11:58:52-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ibWim.506257$2p1.167398@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<4A8AA134.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <9b2m85935hqs6jo6g0g77dderf3b2mfi1e@4ax.com> <4A8AEF2C.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <ps6o85t94agga4379q7888o4qadum5fhhn@4ax.com>`

```
Previous posts/comments snipped.

Some ideas (and samples).

1) If you leave JOBLIB pointing to your "production" versions and ADD a STEP LIB 
statement for each step that needs to access the test module, the search order 
will do what you want (I think).

2) I have ocasssionally, seen JCL PROCs with something like

//JOBLIB DD DSN=&LIB1,DISP=SHR
                 DD DSN=&LIB2,DISP=SHR

where the PROC defaults BOTH the LIB1 and LIB2 symbolics to the same production 
load library.  This allows for an override to a test library for LIB1 while 
still keeping LIB2 pointing to the production library.  The only time this would 
end up with "extra" performance issues would be if you were going to get a S806 
ABEND because the module wasn't in the production library any way.

   * * * * * *

For HIGHLY modularized applications with LOTS of dynamic CALLS, there are 
"performance" issues with the "call search order".  I have (as I recall) seen 
performance improvements by moving a STEPLIB added to make lots of dynamically 
called programs "accessible" early in the search path.  In such cases, however, 
use of some "preload" facility probably works better.

NOTE:
  If you actually will be running anything under ISPF (batch or interactive) be 
aware that ISPLLIB does funny things to the "normal" CALL search order.
```

```
On Wed, 19 Aug 2009 18:17:51 -0600, "Frank Swarbrick"
<Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote:

>>>> On 8/19/2009 at 9:41 AM, in message
><ps6o85t94agga4379q7888o4qadum5fhhn@4ax.com>,
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
>Frank

Sorry for the confusion.  When I said permanent JOBLIB I was indeed
referring to the LNKLST.  But you seem to think you have to include
whatever is defined there when you use a JOBLIB statement in your JCL.
That is not so.  When a job library is in effect, MVS searches it
firstto locate any programs specified in EXEC statements.  If a
program isn't found in the job library, the standard system libraries
are then searched.  This differs, if I recall correctly, from the way
VSE worked.

Regards,
```

```
>>> On 8/20/2009 at 10:06 AM, in message
<4tsq85l42723rnf9uu0mcb3ddg1ql5g62q@4ax.com>,
SkippyPB<swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote:
> On Wed, 19 Aug 2009 18:17:51 -0600, "Frank Swarbrick"
> <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote:
…[45 more quoted lines elided]…
> VSE worked.

Ah, so someone else *does* use the LNKLST for application libraries.  Is
this what you are saying?

Anyway, I was not implying that you have to include LNKLST libraries in the
JOBLIB/STEPLIB.  What I am getting at is that apparently most z/OS shops
don't allow application libraries in LNKLST, and therefore you have to
include them in JOBLIB or STEPLIB so that the system can find them.  This is
what I don't like but am trying to find a solution to in case we decide to
follow the pack, as it were.

I'm not sure how you are thinking that VSE differs from z/OS in this
respect.  z/OS searches JOBLIB/STEPLIB first and LNKLST later.  VSE searches
the TEMP LIBDEF libraries first and the PERM LIBDEF libraries (similar to
LNKLST) later.  In my mind this is the same.  The issue I am referring to on
z/OS would be similar to VSE if we didn't include the application libraries
in the permanent search chain.

Frank
```

## Thread: COBOL rant on "Coding Horror" Blog... — 9 msgs

### COBOL rant on "Coding Horror" Blog...

- **From:** "Kerry Liles" <kerry.removethisandoneperiod.liles@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2009-08-19T09:54:42-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<h6h074$182$1@news.eternal-september.org>`

```
see: http://www.codinghorror.com/blog/archives/001294.html

I regularly read Jeff Atwood's blog and usually find it interesting... ymmv
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL rant on "Coding Horror" Blog...

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-08-20T22:34:02+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7f4n4sF2j9fp3U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<h6h074$182$1@news.eternal-september.org>`

```
Kerry Liles wrote:
> see: http://www.codinghorror.com/blog/archives/001294.html
>
> I regularly read Jeff Atwood's blog and usually find it
> interesting... ymmv

What I found interesting about all these responses was the complete 
bewilderment and lack of understanding, not of COBOL as a language, but of 
the environment it was developed for.

Whatever people may say about it, the main thing of interest to programmers 
is whether or not they can make a living at it. (Otherwise you are indulging 
in "hobby programming"... nothing wrong with that, but recognise it for what 
it is.)

In the last 20 years programmers have seen that possibility get eroded (as 
some of us predicted it would, long before it became obvious) to the point 
where today, a career as a COBOL programmer is no longer viable for a young 
person.

I absolutely loved this quote: "Cobol writers are quite old and are not part 
of our world."

That should be framed and on the wall... :-)

Load a 50 year old language environment onto a brand new Sun server and find 
that "delightfully perverse"?

I find it sad.

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL rant on "Coding Horror" Blog...

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2009-08-20T13:05:35+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<h6jhmu$vm$2@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<h6h074$182$1@news.eternal-september.org> <7f4n4sF2j9fp3U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
In article <7f4n4sF2j9fp3U1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>Kerry Liles wrote:
>> see: http://www.codinghorror.com/blog/archives/001294.html

[snip]

>Whatever people may say about it, the main thing of interest to programmers 
>is whether or not they can make a living at it. (Otherwise you are indulging 
>in "hobby programming"... nothing wrong with that, but recognise it for what 
>it is.)

Mr Dashwood, this appears to require a differentiation between 'work' and 
'play' that some folks try to ignore.  In the USA baseball is called 'The 
National Passtime' and in my experience complaining about one's job takes 
a close second; for a different view I've found (again, a statement of 
mere personal experience) that the more I say 'I'm having Fun!' the more 
others will respond - at times, with moderate confusion - 'But... you're 
doing Good Work.'

[snip]

>I absolutely loved this quote: "Cobol writers are quite old and are not part 
>of our world."

Right up there, I'd say, with 'Never trust anyone over 30', aye.

DD
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL rant on "Coding Horror" Blog...

- **From:** "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net>
- **Date:** 2009-08-20T12:09:40-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<xufjm.32282$rD6.2303@newsfe01.iad>`
- **References:** `<h6h074$182$1@news.eternal-september.org> <7f4n4sF2j9fp3U1@mid.individual.net>`

```

Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:7f4n4sF2j9fp3U1@mid.individual.net...
> Kerry Liles wrote:
> > see: http://www.codinghorror.com/blog/archives/001294.html
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> What I found interesting about all these responses   <snip>

is that the comments from actual Cobol practitioners are so scarce.  If
there are x billion lines of Cobol code being maintained then there surely
must be people doing it; yet the bulk of the comments are from
non-practitioners.  (Bulk ...is ...? Never quite sure about this one!)  I
suspect the practioners are aware of the situation but can't be bothered
commenting about it, because
a) they know that nobody will pay attention - I mean, just because they know
what they're talking about it doesn't mean they are right.  (There's a story
about the poet Robert Frost  sitting in on a class discussing his work.  The
professor did his thing and Frost rose to point out that he was the author
of the poems and disagreed.  The professor is said to have retorted that
"that doesn't mean you know anything about them!")
b) they are tired of being dissed for what is after all an essential part of
the business
c) most of them are not the clueless dinosaurs who can't even use a screen
that are so often instanced
d) their critics have their own weaknesses, but since I/T is the one
profession where the more you know the less you are believed the Cobol
practioners are by definition unworthy to be included in the discussion

and so forth.

Inevitable  things will inevitably happen.  No point in gloating over them.

PL
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL rant on "Coding Horror" Blog...

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-08-21T13:34:09+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7f6bsjF2jnegqU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<h6h074$182$1@news.eternal-september.org> <7f4n4sF2j9fp3U1@mid.individual.net> <xufjm.32282$rD6.2303@newsfe01.iad>`

```
tlmfru wrote:
> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:7f4n4sF2j9fp3U1@mid.individual.net...
…[33 more quoted lines elided]…
> them.

Personally, I have been saddened by the demise of COBOL for some years now, 
but it had to happen. The world has moved on and the COBOL community simply 
didn't. I don't think anyone is gloating over it, Peter.

It is very hard to work daily with a language, achieve success and joy with 
it and NOT get emotionally attached to it.

But, all of it, even the languages-du-jour are simply computer software and 
getting emotionally attached to them is as sensible as falling in love with 
your toaster... :-)

I am finding the new challenges in web development, code generation, and 
legacy migration which I am currently undertaking, to be every bit as 
challenging and satisfying as COBOL application development always was for 
me. To a programmer, there is always intense satisfaction in making 
something work, and this seems to transcend any particular language. I am 
absolutely blown away by new packages like Iron Speed (simply amazing for 
web development), Stimulsoft (Crystal Reports on steroids and without the 
complexity), and even the latest incarnation of my own Toolset, which is 
being very well received and bringing COBOL legacy into today's world in a 
fraction of the time it would take to do so manually.
(OO COBOL is an essential part of this transition, and will be for some 
years yet...)

In the old days we wrote a solution for every problem; nowadays the 
solutions are there to a large extent and the challenge is how to employ 
them to best advantage. Class Libraries like .NET (currently 80,000 
"functions" available), and the latest Java Packages, mean that there is no 
longer a need to build everything from scratch and solutions can be provided 
in a fraction of the time that was once the case.

But it's still fun to sit back and say those three magic words.... "Start 
'er up!"

Pete.
```

## Thread: COBOL versions Mainframe — 4 msgs

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe)_

### COBOL versions Mainframe

- **From:** MainframeKobold <Juergen.knauff@commerzbank.com>
- **Date:** 2009-08-25T01:46:07-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b018ce6a-6938-4161-a527-274612fbdb32@j21g2000yqe.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hi,

When i'm looking in 'google' about Cobol Versions, i find a big
confusion and no clear different Host <> PC.


Know anyone a reliable Listing from Cobol-Versions in the IBM-
Mainframe-
Environment ?


Specially I need to know, since when and in which version COBOL know
the statements 'XML Parse' and 'XML Generate'


thanks for answers !
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL versions Mainframe

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2009-08-25T10:52:07-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<JMTkm.703452$641.272412@en-nntp-10.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<b018ce6a-6938-4161-a527-274612fbdb32@j21g2000yqe.googlegroups.com>`

```
XML PARSE was introduced in Enterprise COBOL V3R1, see:
  http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/IGY3LR10/FRONT_1.6.2

XML GENERATE was introduced in Enterprise COBOL V3R3, see:
    http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/IGY3LR20/PREFACE.8.1

The most current (MVS, OS/390, z/OS) compiler is Enterprise COBOL V4R1, see:
  http://publibfp.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr/Shelves/igy3sh40

If you actually do want a history of all the releases of IBM COBOL, there is a 
page, but I can't find it quickly.

NOTE:
   DOS and VSE COBOL's are a separate issue.
```

```
> XML PARSE was introduced in Enterprise COBOL V3R1, see:

…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> page, but I can't find it quickly.

One minor [sorta] change to Bill's response.  Announced about the time 
Bill wrote his response:  Ent. COBOL V4.2.  Shipments began Friday, 
28-Aug-2009.

And, just FWIW, since the question was specifically about XML 
processing, there are a number of enhancements in V4.2 for XML in 
addition to other significant enhancements.  The easiest access to this 
new info is via:
> http://www-01.ibm.com/software/awdtools/cobol/zos/
 From there you can see the Announcement and the COBOL Library for the 
new release.

Should you want to look at other platforms, then the 'COBOL Family' page:
> http://www-01.ibm.com/software/awdtools/cobol/
would be the place to start.
```

```
vague "implement Cobol 2002 features", which I doubt will ever happen in
whole.)

implemented?  Two offhand I would like to see are "FALSE VALUE" for 88
levels (from Cobol 2002) and from the upcoming(!) standard, FUNCTION TRIM.

Wouldn't it be great to see EC 4.3 with perhaps just a bunch of small nice
improvements?  I am surprised 4.2 came out so soon after 4.1, and even more
surprised (pleasantly!) to see some requirements met (BLOCK0 compiler option
and CICS precompiler options listed).

Frank
```

```
 Ron (and comp.lang.cobol),
  No, there is no exiting SHARE requirement for the "TRIM" intrinsic function 
included in the *draft* of the next COBOL revision.  I could be mistaken, but I 
don't think there are any requirements for ANY of the features that are in the 
draft but not in the '02 Standard (although - at least one "function-pointer" is 
already in Enterprise COBOL).

Given the past rate of delivery on "nice to have" features from the '02 
Standard, I don't know how soon you could expect those from the draft revision. 
On the other hand, given the enhancements related to XML, it certainly would be 
POSSIBLE that IBM would implement the TRIM function "in our own life time" - but 
only when/if someone actually submits a requirement for it.
```

```
''I did that,' says my memory.  'I could not have done that,' says my 
pride, and remains inexorable. Eventually -- the memory yields.'

F. W. Nietzsche, 'Beyond Good and Evil'

Philosophy aside... even when folks were coding such things I recall not 
only being VERY STRONGLY ADVISED not to do such but there were sites that 
used the Forbidden Words 'precompile', through which source was run and 
immediately turned back to the developer if it contained the wrong stuff.

>But standards can be created and enforced for new code.

Rare, indeed, is the 'new program'; most Big Iron shops spend the majority 
of their budgets on maintenance/enhancement.  What happens when an 
internal table is enlarged (OCCURS 200 gets bumped up to OCCURS 500) and 
the coder gets told 'Oh... now you have to change all the GO TO -EXIT to 
EXIT PARAGRAPH'?  My guess is that this would not be a happy-making event; 
a more proper approach might be to form a team (with a rotating roster so 
nobody gets truly stuck in that particular barrel) and go through the 
libraries, program by program, making the modifications, testing, et 
cetera...

... but that requires foresight, organisational skills and Costs Money.

DD
```

## Thread: Call for participation - SLE 2009 — 1 msgs

### Call for participation - SLE 2009

- **From:** "A.Serebrenik@tue.nl" <A.Serebrenik@tue.nl>
- **Date:** 2009-08-28T00:04:34-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<65c641ee-7ce9-42c3-bc03-3f1507c406bb@r42g2000yqj.googlegroups.com>`

```
___________________________________________________________

                  Call for Participation - SLE 2009

 2nd International Conference on Software Language Engineering

                     http://planet-sl.org/sle2009
                 Denver, Colorado, October 5-6, 2009



____________________________________________________________

           Co-located with 12th International Conference on
    Model-Driven Engineering Languages and Systems (MODELS 2009)
                and 8th International Conference on
     Generative Programming and Component Engineering (GPCE 2009)


Highlights:
- Research Program:   15 research papers, 6 short papers and 2 tool
demos
                      (program listed below)
- Keynote Speakers:   Jean Bezivin and James Cordy
- Early Registration: Open until Sept 14, 2008


Conference
----------

The 2nd International Conference on Software Language Engineering
(SLE)
is devoted to topics related to artificial languages in software
engineering. SLE's foremost mission is to encourage and organize
communication between communities that have traditionally looked
at software languages from different, more specialized, and yet
complementary perspectives. SLE emphasizes the fundamental notion
of languages as opposed to any realization in specific "technical
spaces". SLE 2009 will be co-located with the 12th IEEE/ACM
International Conference on Model-Driven Engineering Languages and
Systems (MODELS 2009) and  8th International Conference on
Generative Programming and Component Engineering (GPCE 2009).



Scope
-----

The term 'software language' comprises all sorts of artificial
languages used in software development, including general-purpose
programming languages, domain-specific languages, modeling and
meta-modeling languages, data models, and ontologies. Used in its
broadest sense, examples include modeling languages such as
UML-based and domain-specific modeling languages, business process
modeling languages, and web application modeling languages.  The
term 'software language' also comprises APIs and collections of
design patterns that are implicitly defined languages.

Software language engineering is the application of a systematic,
disciplined, quantifiable approach to the development, use, and
maintenance of these languages. Thus, the SLE conference is
concerned with all phases of the life cycle of software languages;
these include the design, implementation, documentation, testing,
deployment, evolution, recovery, and retirement of languages. Of
special interest are tools, techniques, methods and formalisms that
support these activities. In particular, tools are often based on
or even automatically generated from a formal description of the
language. Hence, of special interest is the treatment of language
descriptions as software artifacts, akin to programs - while paying
attention to the special status of language descriptions, subject
to tailored engineering principles and methods for modularization,
refactoring, refinement, composition, versioning, co-evolution,
and analysis.


Accepted Papers
---------------

Research Papers
    * August Schwerdfeger and Eric Van Wyk. Verifiable Parse
       Table Composition for Deterministic Parsing
    * Frédéric Mallet, Francois Lagarde, Charles André, Sebastien
       Gerard and Francois Terrier. An Automated Process for
       implementing Multi-level domain models
    * Krzysztof Czarnecki, Thiago Tonelli Bartolomei, Ralf Laemmel
       and Tijs van der Storm. Transcript of an API migration for
       two XML APIs
    * Lukas Renggli, Marcus Denker and Oscar Nierstrasz.
       Language Boxes: Bending the Host Language with Modular
       Language Changes
    * Maartje de Jonge, Emma Nilsson-Nyman, Lennart C. L. Kats
       and Eelco Visser. Natural and Flexible Error Recovery for
       Generated Parsers
    * Markus Herrmannsdoerfer, Daniel Ratiu and Guido Wachsmuth.
       Language Evolution in Practice: The History of GMF
    * Mathieu Acher, Philippe Collet, Philippe Lahire and
       Robert France.  Composing Feature Models
    * Mauricio Alferez, João Santos, Ana Moreira, Alessandro
       Garcia, Uirá Kulesza, João Araújo and Vasco Amaral.
       Multi-View Composition Language for Software Product
       Line Requirements
    * Nils Thieme, Christian Wende and Steffen Zschaler.
       A Role-based Approach Towards Modular Language
       Engineering
    * Steffen Zschaler, Dimitrios Kolovos, Nicholas Drivalos,
       Richard Paige and Awais Rashid. Domain-Specific
       Metamodelling Languages for Software Language Engineering
    * Steffen Zschaler, Pablo Sanchez, Joao Santos, Mauricio
       Alferez, Awais Rashid, Lidia Fuentes, Ana Moreira, Joao
       Araujo and Uira Kulesza. VML* -- A Family of Languages
       for Variability Management in Software Product Lines
    * Tihamer Levendovszky, Anantha Narayanan, Daniel
       Balasubramanian and Gabor Karsai. A Novel Approach to
       Semi-Automated Evolution of DSML Model Transformation
    * Tim Bauer and Martin Erwig.  Declarative Scripting in
       Haskell
    * Uwe Jugel. Generating Smart Wrapper Libraries for Arbitrary
       APIs
    * Zef Hemel and Eelco Visser. PIL: A Platform Independent
       Language for Retargetable DSLs

Short papers

    * Anya Helene Bagge. Yet Another Language Extension Scheme
    * Daniel Spiewak and Tian Zhao.  ScalaQL: Language-Integrated
       Database Queries for Scala
    * Danny M. Groenewegen and Eelco Visser. Integration of Data
       Validation and User Interface Concerns in a DSL for Web
       Applications
    * Ivan Kurtev and Alfons Laarman. Ontological Metamodeling with
       Explicit Instantiation
    * Jerónimo Irazábal and Claudia Pons. Model transformation
       languages relying on models as ADTs
    * Paul Laird and Stephen Barrett. Towards the Dynamic
       Evolution of Domain Specific Languages

Tools

    * Elina Kalnina, Audris Kalnins, Edgars Celms and Agris
       Sostaks. Graphical template language for transformation
       synthesis
    * Florian Heidenreich, Jendrik Johannes, Mirko Seifert and
       Christian Wende. Closing the Gap between Modelling and Java

------------
```
